Table 5.2 presents the names and defining characteristics for the adapted
heuristics, which will be subject to further tuning and testing. Both methods
share the same capacity scaling procedure, but differ in terms of diversification
procedure. RCSF will refer to the method employing the marginal cost driven
diversification, while RINVF will use inverse optimisation to move the search
trajectory through the solution space.
5.6
Parameter tuning
Parameter tuning for the route-restricted methods, RCSF and RINVF, follows
the usual dynamical approach. As they both apply capacity scaling, they share
parameters λ, B, dB, Bmin and dL, but differ in what parameters controls the
diversification procedures.
Due to the design of the route-based capacity scaling, Algorithm 5.2.1, the
smoothing parameter interval λ∈[0.25−2δ, 0.25+δ], δ<0.05 is early identified as
the most stable region, in terms of obtaining feasible solutions. Further, dB=15,
Bmin=10 and dL=0.01 provide the immediate best performance across all prob-
lem sizes, provided by the Canad-instances. During the tuning and tests con-
ducted in this thesis, these values will be fixed, as well as the usual B=75.
It should be noted that the rapid decrease in B, i.e. the large value for dB, is
mostly appropriate to also handle the smallest instances. Many of these are how-
ever trivial, efficiently solved, problems, so if one is willing to reduce the stability
of the method, for smaller instances, it could be appropriate to reconsider these
values.
Finally, one has that the lower bounds available for the route-restricted version
of MCF are generally weaker than the best known for the original problem. This
means that the results will appear to be of worse quality, than the actual reality.
5.6.1
RCSF
When tuning the RCSF method, the considered parameter-grid is defined by the
parameters λ, preject, ndiv and maxfree. The threshold for number of free edges
obtained through filtering, maxfree, is considered at the usual level, maxfree=75,
as well as at the higher maxfree=85 and maxfree=100.
The degree of marginal-cost based diversification, determined by preject and ndiv,
is considered within the same range as for the original methods using this mea-
sure.
==PAGE:95==
5.6 Parameter tuning
87
The performance of each tested point in the parameter grid is summarised
in Figure F.1-F.3 in Appendix F.1. It is evident that the worst case behaviour
is generally stable across the parameter section, both in terms of running time
and maximal solution gap. The method also appears to reach the time-out for
at least one instance for almost all parameter combinations.
There are however some parameter settings which distinguish themselves in terms
of significantly better maximal solution gaps. The fact that these settings are
located in separate regions of the grid, surrounded by parameter combinations
with poor performance, does however imply that the method in general has less
potential.

RINVF
The inverse-cost based diversification used in RINVF means that the parameters
considered for tuning mainly consists of the capacity scaling smoothing parameter
λ and the weight of the unit-flow variables in the inverse optimisation problem wx.
In addition to this, different thresholds maxfree≥75 for the maximal obtained
free edges, are considered.
The usual summary of parameter-combination performance is given for each
tested grid-point, in Figure F.4-F.5 in Appendix F.2.
The parameter setting
which have a reported maximal gap of 666%, are those where the capacity scal-
ing was not successful in finding a feasible solution for at least one of the instances.
These all occur for λ=0.3, proving the unreliability of the procedure when the
smoothing parameter is increased.
As seen for CSINVF, the running times are generally slow, independently of the
threshold maxfree, but the lowest threshold naturally has some advantage when
considering this performance indicator. The shortest average running times are
==PAGE:96==
5.6 Parameter tuning
88
further found when wx=0.0. This is likely due to the fact that with this setting,
there is no weight given to the change in the unit-flow cost-vector for the indi-
vidual commodities, meaning a larger absolute perturbation is expected from the
inverse optimisation problem. The larger change in cost-vector will lead to larger
moves in the solution space, and is thus likely to lead to more free edges faster.
The drawback of many of the fastest parameter settings, is that they generally
have large worst case solution gaps, even if the average achieved is at a relatively
good level.

==PAGE:97==
CHAPTER 6
Results for FCMCF
For each of the filtering heuristics, several parameter-sets have been selected. Dif-
ferent parameter combinations define separate versions of the considered heuristic.
In this section, the performance of all selected versions will be considered across
a wider range of problem instances.
Due to the span of different running times across the methods, it is chosen to pair
them with Local Branching. This is done by; if termination happens before one
hour has passed, Local branching is applied to allow for further improvements of
the heuristically obtained solution within the prescribed time-limit.
The parameters chosen for Local branching are M=10, δM=2 and T=1000 sec-
onds. The choice of parameters is based on those used by Katayama et.al. [11]
and some initial testing, as well as the observation that the upper limit of one
hour imposes great restrictions on the call to the Local Branching. In the liter-
ature significantly longer running times were allowed, which is also why M=20
was a plausible choice there. As the entire filtering heuristic and Local Branching
together are to be restricted by one hour of running time, it is necessary to use
smaller values for M and T.
The effect of adding the Local Branching is interesting in terms of the perfor-
mance of different versions of the same method, defined by different parameter
settings. For each of the filtering heuristics, several parameter combinations were
chosen based on the tuning results. The selection criteria used were based on both
solution quality and running time.
The parameter combinations with faster running times are those most likely to
achieve improved performance due to the pairing with Local Branching. However,
the performance of Local Branching is also highly dependant upon a good initial
solution. This means that having more time available for Local Branching is not
necessarily the most beneficial factor for obtaining good-quality final solutions.
For assessing solution qualities, a combination of tightest known lower bound,
LB, and the best known upper bound, UB, for each instance will be considered.
The appropriate bounds are provided in the literature and the performance in
relation to these bounds will be measured in the LB-gap and UB-gap respectively.
LB-gap=100%
Obj −LB
LB

and UB-gap=100%
Obj −UB
UB

(6.1)
As such, one has that the LB-gap gives the maximal percentage distance to the
unknown, true optimal solution. The UB-gap gives the percentage distance to
the the best known solution of an instance or the percentage improvement. A
==PAGE:98==
6.1 Best known bounds
90
negative UB-gap will therefore imply that a new best known solution is found.
Best case for the LB-gap is that it is 0%.
6.1
Best known bounds
Considering the general fixed-cost multicommodity flow problem, and the Canad
problem-instances, the tightest lower and upper bounds are provided by Katayama
et.al. in [10] and [11]. The best upper bounds are achieved by the path-formulation
based Capacity Scaling. Further for the non-trivial instances tighter upper bounds
are given obtained by running Local Branching, starting from the solutions ob-
tained by capacity scaling.
For the GT-instances the best known bound are reported by Hewitt et.al.[5].
The best known solutions to these instances are found by a proposed IP-search
heuristic. Additionally, both the best found upper and lower bounds obtained
by CPLEX after 12 hours are also provided in the literature. Further, they also
obtained stronger upper bounds by an IP-search heuristic. Therefore, the solu-
tion quality of these instances will be assessed both based on the tightest known
lower bounds and the best found upper bounds presented in this article [5].
In addition to comparing the obtained solutions to the tightest upper and lower
bounds provided in the literature, a reference method is constructed by pairing
the edge-formulation based capacity scaling heuristic with the cycle-based intensi-
fication procedure and Local Branching. The resulting method will be referred to
as CSLB, which will be tested on all available instance with a one hour time-limit.
6.2
Overall results
For each of the filtering heuristics, a subset of 2-4 parameter settings were chosen
during the tuning phase. The different value-sets were selected based on different
criteria believed to be promising performance indicators.
The heuristics, paired with Local Branching, have been tested on all available
Canad- and GT-instances for each of these selected parameter-sets. Based on
the results, a single parameter setting is chosen for each of the heuristics. The
selected parameter combination for a heuristic defines the main version of the
considered method. These versions will be considered when comparing the differ-
ent heuristics.
The instance specific results obtained by each version of each heuristic are listed
in Appendix J.

6.2.2
General results
Included in the in-depth analyses for each heuristic, Appendix G.1-G.9, are in-
vestigations of how instance characteristics like size, fixed cost level, relative to
unit-flow cost, and capacity tightness.
It is found that the trends are similar across most methods. Firstly, the fixed
cost level is the most detrimental for performance for all the filtering heuristics,
where performance is poorer if the fixed-cost level is high. Further, for the C-
and R-category instances, the heuristics generally perform better for instances
with either high fixed-cost level and tight constraints, or low fixed-cost level and
loose constraints.
For the C+-category instances performance is worse for instances with high fixed-
cost levels and tight capacities. These are also instances for which Katayama et
al. reported poorer solution qualities and longer running times.
Considering the GT-instances, there is a general consensus amongst the methods,
that the instances with high fixed-cost levels and loose capacities are significantly
more challenging. The results for SSS1F and SSS4F exhibit the most even results
for either capacity-tightness when used to solve these instances. There are how-
ever still indications that it is easier to obtain good solutions for the instances
with tight capacities, especially when compared to the best known upper bounds
reported by Hewitt et al. [5].
For exact results for each method, the reader is referred to the referenced appen-
dices.
What is also unveiled during tuning is that the time-control measures im-
plemented are not sufficient in terms of appropriately terminating the heuristics
==PAGE:100==
6.2 Overall results
92
after one hour.
Especially for the larger instances, some of the running times used exceed the
prescribed hour by a significant amount. This is in most cases a consequence of
the lack of time-control measures in the intensification procedure. Some effort
was made to control this for the larger instances, but they were not successful in
ensuring timely termination throughout all tests.
Another important factor influencing running times, was the setting of the
threshold for free edges, maxfree. It was found that for several of the methods,
that the iterative phase required most of the running time, and that many free
edges lead to time-outs when solving the final restricted MIP.
For the inverse optimisation based methods, different values for maxfree were
tested, discovering that larger values could contribute to improved performance.
The fact that maxfree=75 was fixed for the marginal cost based methods could
therefore mean their potential is not fully utilised. One did however have that
most of these methods resulted in more free edges than the prescribed threshold,
on average. This was found to be a consequence of the marginal-cost based di-
versification having a significant effect.
Further, the marginal cost based methods were also found to experience time-
outs, due to a large number of free edges. Therefore, it is believed that for most
of the methods, increasing the threshold would not lead to significant improve-
ments in terms of obtaining better solution qualities.
What also made the larger threshold more appropriate for the inverse optimisa-
tion based procedures, was the overall observation that the applied diversifica-
tion was slower in obtaining free edges and stagnated throughout the iterative
process. The stagnation meant that for the larger threshold to be advantageous,
additional termination criteria were needed to stop in cases of stagnation. The
average number of free edges across the instances was still lower than for the
marginal cost-based methods, but for instances where obtaining more free edges
was possible, these measures improved the final results.
The threshold for number of free edges is as such an example of a heuristic
control measure, which could be considered for further testing. In addition, there
are several other important control mechanisms, such as run-time limits, itera-
tion limits and speed-up, for which further adjustments could be advantageous.
Even though some testing has been conducted to determine appropriate choices,
they have not been subject to the same thorough tuning as some of the other
parameters.
As the filtering heuristics depend upon other regulative measures than just
the parameters considered in the tuning, there might be other, undiscovered,
settings which are more beneficial.
Therefore, in addition to comparing the main versions of each heuristic, the cross-
parameter performances will also be considered. This refers to considering the
best solution obtained by a heuristic, for each instance, across all the tested
parameter-sets. The motivation behind this comparison is that for several of the
methods, multiple parameter-sets exhibit promising results and choosing a single,
best performing value-set proved to be challenging.
==PAGE:101==
6.3 Comparing methods
93
Further, the cross-parameter comparisons might help indicate whether a method
is promising for further research, even though the chosen main version tested,
cannot match this performance.
6.3

The comparison of the different filtering heuristics will be considered in two parts,
divided into Canad- and GT-instances. Based on the two comparisons, the most
promising heuristics will be highlighted and their performances will be subject
to more in-depth analysis.

6.3 Comparing methods

are known to be close the available lower bounds is reported. Such small LB-gaps
indicate that the solutions found are very close to being guaranteed optimal and
when LB-gap<0.01% the obtained solution will be considered as approximately
optimal.
Firstly, it can be observed that after the application of Local Branching, the
reference method CSLB outperforms both SSF and CSF, with regards to most
performance measures. SSF does however obtain a few more approximately op-
timal solutions than CSLB.
The comparisons further show that SSS4F has the overall best heuristic per-
formance, as it achieves the best solution quality levels before Local Branching
is applied. Further, one has that, in terms of LB-gap, SSS1F obtains the overall
best solution quality after Local Branching is applied, while SSS3F results in the
best average and worst case UB-gaps.
SSS4F achieves the greatest relative improvement of solution quality, compared
to Katayama, as it has the smallest minimal UB-gap. SSS1F achieves the second
best improvement while also having the second best worst case behaviour.
Lastly, SSS3F is best in terms of how the individual solutions compare to the
literature, as well in terms of the count of instances with near optimal solutions.

than for both CINV1F and S3INVF.
The best heuristic solution qualities are obtained by CINV2F, where espe-

best of the marginal-cost based methods, in terms of this measure.

after Local Branching, but the most improved solutions belong to CINV1F and

Compared to the individual solutions obtained by Katayama, CINV2F also re-
sults in both more improved and fewer impaired solutions.
Figure H.17 and H.18 in Appendix H.12.1 compares the distribution of the
obtained UB-gaps, for each of the filtering heuristics, excluding SSF and CSF.
The first figure considers the distribution across all Canad-instances, while only
the instances for which the solutions differ between the heuristics are considered
in the second figure.
It is evident that applying Local Branching is a determining factor in obtain-
ing overall levelled results across the heuristics. This can be seen as CSLB and
S3INVF which generally have much poorer performances in isolation, approaches
the same solution qualities as the other methods when considering the final so-
lutions. These two heuristics are also generally faster than the others, meaning
the Local Branching phase is allowed more time.
Further, one has that the maximal recorded UB-gap for CINV1F is due to a
single outlier, whereas the rest of the solutions obtained are about the same level
as for the other heuristics. Much the same observation can be made for S3INVF.
In general, there seems to be few instances for which the heuristics have poor
performance.

GT-instances
The performances of the different methods are compared for the GT-instances,
in a similar manner as for the Canad-instances. Less focus is however centred
around the LB-gaps, as the tightest known lower bounds are rather weak for
these instances. The comparisons will therefore emphasise the obtained UB-gaps,
comparing the solutions to those reported by Hewitt et al. [5].

improvements are also of greater magnitude.

6.4 Cross parameter performances
98
6.4
Cross parameter performances
As mentioned before, the results for the Canad-instances reported by Katayama
et al., are based on the best obtained solutions for each instance, testing the
path-formulation based capacity scaling heuristic and Local Branching for differ-
ent parameters. Therefore, this section will also provide comparisons of cross-
parameter performance for some of the more promising filtering methods. The
parameter sets considered for each method are those selected during the tuning
phase of the concerned heuristic.
These results are considered both to provide a more fair comparison to the liter-
ature of the proposed filtering heuristics, as well as to consider the potential of
each method on a more generic level. A heuristic might have beneficial qualities,
which are not sufficiently represented by the exact chosen design of the method.

final solutions are considered, both in terms of maximal distance to optimum and
how they compare to the solutions reported in the literature.
An interesting result is that all four presented heuristics on average perform
better than the heuristic proposed by Katayama et al. Firstly, one has that the
average UB-gap is slightly negative for all methods. Further, the largest achieved
improvements, i.e. the minimal UB-gaps, are of much greater magnitude than
the maximal UB-gaps. SSS3F is however the only heuristic for which the count
of instances with improved solutions is higher than the count of worse solutions.
SSS3F is also the heuristic, out of the four, which has the lowest average UB-
and LB-gap. The best worst case performance is obtained by CINV2F, which is
also the performance measure for which the four heuristic differ the most.
The performance for the Canad-instances is here the most interesting, as Katayama
et al. also reported their results in terms of the best cross-parameter solutions.
Hewitt et al. used one version of their IP-search heuristic when solving the GT-
instances, so the basis for comparison is less fair here.

Table 6.7 similarly summarises the cross-parameter performance of the best

is presented instead of CINV2F, as it proved to have better performance for these
larger instances. The cross-parameter results do however indicate that CINV1F
is not very beneficial either when compared to the best heuristics.
The results for the GT-instances further differ from the Canad-instances, in that
SSS3F performs poorly. One does however have that both SSS1F and SSS4F
performs relatively well. Compared to the solutions from the literature, SSS1F
obtains improved solutions for two thirds of the instances, and SSS4F improves
the solutions for three quarters of the instances.
As such, the comparisons of cross-parameter performances maintain the re-
sults from before, in terms of which of the filtering heuristics provide the best
solution qualities for the different instances.
What is compelling however, is
that all four presented heuristics exhibit very good performance for the Canad-
instances, in comparison to the literature. For the GT-instances, there are also
two heuristics which obtain competitive solutions.
Further, the most surprising observation here is that the results indicate a greater
potential for CINV2F than what has been seen before. One might therefore be
inclined to believe the possibility that there exists a version of this procedure
which also has a comparatively good performance.
6.5
Distance to optimal solutions
The conducted comparisons of heuristic performances have revealed that the best
performing methods are SSS1F, SSS3F, and SSS4F. It is found that SSS3F is es-
pecially promising for the smaller Canad-instances, while SSS1F and SSS4F are
more robust in terms of also producing good-quality solutions for the significantly
larger GT-instances.
The comparisons to the solutions from the literature has focused on the relative
distance from the heuristically obtained solutions to those reported in the litera-
ture. This chapter will therefore finish by comparing the best filtering heuristics
to the methods from the literature in terms of the LB-gaps.
==PAGE:108==

Figure 6.1: Comparing the LB-gap distributions for the most promising filtering
heuristics to that obtained by Katayama et al. [11] for a subset of the Canad-

for which all considered methods obtain the optimal solution are excluded.
Figure 6.1 compares the distribution of the LB-gaps obtained by the best per-
forming filtering heuristics to the corresponding distribution from the literature.
The instances for which all heuristics locate the optimal solutions are excluded
both to better illustrate how the performances of the methods differ and in an
attempt to ignore the more trivial instances.
The left plot considers the main versions of the filtering heuristics, meaning all
solutions are found using the parameter settings specified in Table 6.1. The right
plot considers the cross-parameter performances.
The overall results are much the same for both plots.
All three filtering
heuristics have poorer worst case solution qualities than what is reported in the
literature. The main bodies of the distributions do however indicate that the
three filtering heuristics perform better for most of the considered instances, and
their distributions skew more towards zero. One also has that the median for
these distributions approaches a 0% LB-gap, implying that near optimal solu-
tions are found for most instances considered.
Figure 6.2 similarly compares the LB-gap distributions of SSS1F and SSS4F
to that obtained in the literature for the GT-instances. The cross-parameter com-
parison is also included here, but is less fair as a basis for comparing the filtering
methods to the IP-search proposed by Hewitt et al. [5].
It is evident from either plot that both SSS1F and SSS4F have obtained compar-
atively good solutions to the instances. The differences between the distributions
are not very prominent, but there are indications of the worst case behaviour
being worse for the solutions from the literature.
One further has that the tightest known lower bounds for these instances are
generally weak, so it will be more difficult to say something definite about the
performances based on the LB-gap. However, as both SSS1F and SSS4F also
proved to on average improve the solutions from the literature, there are indica-

6.6 Overall results

heuristics, to that obtained by Hewitt et al. [5] across all GT-instances.

6.6
Overall results
The overall results from comparing the different filtering heuristics are that the
heuristics utilising a smoothed slope scaling scheme and the marginal cost based
diversification procedure appear to perform the best. Especially, the fact that
SSS1F and SSS4F obtain some of the best levels of solutions qualities across both
the Canad- and GT-instances are considered promising.
SSS3F resulted in overall better solutions for the smaller Canad-instances, but
proved to be inadequate in terms of it not being able to handle the larger GT-
instances. SSS1F and SSS4F also achieved good-quality solution levels for the
Canad-instances and proved to be better equipped to maintain a comparatively
good performance for the larger instance.
As such, the heuristics SSS1F and SSS4F can be promoted as the most ro-
bust, well-performing filtering heuristics proposed for solving the fixed-cost multi-
commodity flow problem. They have proven to perform well for a wide range of
different instances, compared to the best known solutions from the literature.
Several of the other filtering heuristics also had some promising results and dur-
ing testing new best solutions have been found for several of the considered
instances.
==PAGE:110==
CHAPTER 7
Results for RFCMCF
This chapter will present and compare the results obtained when testing the
methods for the route-restricted multicommodity flow problem, described and
tuned in Chapter 5. The methods, RCSF and RINVF, are tested on all Canad-
instances, for each of their selected parameter settings.
The maximal running time is constrained to one hour, as in the tuning. If the
heuristic terminates before this time-limit is reached, Local Branching, starting
from the heuristic solution, is applied for the remaining time.
In addition to comparing the obtained solutions to the tightest upper and lower
bounds provided by CPLEX, a reference method is constructed by pairing the
route-restricted capacity scaling and local branching, which will be referred to as
RCSLB. RCSLB is also restricted by the one hour time-limit.
7.1
RCSLB
RCSLB consists of the route-restricted capacity scaling with λ=0.25 in addition
to the usual B=75, Bmin=10, dB=15 and dL=0.01, followed by Local branching
using M=10, δM=2 and T=1000 seconds.
The detailed instance specific results for all Canad-instances are given in Table
J.113-J.115 in Appendix J.29.
It is found that for 159 of the 196 considered
instances, the solutions obtained are either equally as good, or better than the
upper bounds found by CPLEX’s MIP-solver. This subset of instances consists
of 18 from the C-category, 9 from the C+-category and 132 for the R-category.
Table 7.1 summarises the performance of RCSLB across the Canad-instances.
The overall performance across all instances is considered, in addition to category-
specific results. Further, the C+- and R-category problems are grouped by size,
as treated by Katayama et al. [11]. It can be observed that the route-restricted
capacity scaling has generally poor performance, as both the average LB gaps
and average UB gaps are relatively large, across all considered groupings. This is
likely to be a consequence of how the scaling heuristic is forced to concern itself
with both obtaining integer feasibility, and good quality solutions for the route-
restricted problem. The increased effort towards obtaining feasibility, might come
at the expense of solution quality.The running time is however generally fast.
This means that the Local Branching phase is allocated a significant amount of
time in most cases, providing greater potential for improved solutions.
The final LB and UB gaps reported for RCSLB further emphasises this fact,
as significant reductions are obtained in the Local Branching phase, compared to

the Scaling heuristic alone. The average overall LB gap is reduced from 4.69% to
only 0.6%. The group with the poorest performance is the C+-category subset
of larger instances, c+7-c+12, for which the average gap is reduced from 23.34%
to 4.07%.
The maximal overall UB solution gap is 5.78%, which is relatively high. However,
the upper bounds were obtained by CPLEX’s MIP-solver, allowing for one hour
running time and multithreading up to 32 threads.
This means that the use
of computational resources was significantly greater when obtaining the bounds.
As such, the RCSLB does succeed in finding relatively good solutions with little
computational effort.
Table I.1-I.3 in Appendix I.1 presents the performance of RCSLB grouped by
the different instance characteristics. The instances are each defined by a level
of fixed cost, relative to the unit-flow costs, and the tightness of capacities.
The results show that RCS alone generally locates better solutions for instances
with a low fixed-cost level and tight capacities. Further, after Local Branching
is applied, the difference in performance between tight and loose capacities is
reduced, but the differences between fixed-cost ratios remain.
7.2
RCSF
The tuning of RCSF resulted in three sets of parameters, chosen for further
testing. Roughly, one has that (λ, preject, ndiv, maxfree)=(0.25, 0.3, 20, 100) was
chosen based on resulting in the best worst case solution quality for the training
set instances.
Further, (λ, preject, ndiv, maxfree)=(0.25, 0.2, 5, 100) was chosen
for resulting in a better average solution gap, as well as having a relatively good
worst case gap. Lastly, (λ, preject, ndiv, maxfree)=(0.25, 0.22, 5, 85) was chosen
based on computational time, while also resulting in decent solution qualities.
==PAGE:112==
7.2 RCSF
104
The hope is that the beneficial traits of these parameter settings, when tested
on the training-set, will translate to all available instances. Further, it also re-
mains to see how the additional application of Local Branching, for the remainder
of the allowed hour of running time, will affect how the different parameter set-
tings of RCSF compares.
7.2.1
Comparing parameters
The instance specific results for the different parameter settings of SSS1F are
given in Tables J.116-J.124 in Appendix J.30.1-J.30.3.

mance in terms of solution qualities, while (preject, ndiv, maxfree) = (0.22, 5, 85)
generally has the best worst case behaviour. The first of these settings also re-
sults in a significantly larger number of improved solutions.
The benefit of (preject, ndiv, maxfree) = (0.22, 5, 85), in spite of having the low-
est count of improved solutions, is that the average performance is close to the
overall best. Further, one has that the difference between the worst case per-
formances is relatively large, where (preject, ndiv, maxfree) = (0.22, 5, 85) stays
within a 10.35% distance of the tightest known lower gaps, while the next best
parameter setting, (preject, ndiv, maxfree) = (0.3, 20, 100), has a reported maxi-
mum of 12.32%.
==PAGE:113==
7.2 RCSF
105
Based on these results, the version of RCSF chosen for further investigations
and performance comparisons is defined by the parameters (preject, ndiv, maxfree) =
(0.22, 5, 85).
7.2.2

Testing RCSF(λ=0.25, preject=0.22, ndiv=5, maxfree=85) paired with Local
Branching on the 196 available Canad-instances, resulted in equally as good
or improved solutions for 151 instances, compared to the upper bounds found
by CPLEXs MIP-solver. This subset consists of 19 C-category instances, 9 C+-
category instances and 123 R-category instances.

Table 7.3 summarises the solution qualities obtained for the route-restricted
Canad-instances, when using RCSF(λ=0.25, preject=0.22, ndiv=5, maxfree=85).
One can observe that the smaller instances, c+1-c+6 and r01-r09, are solved
to optimality. The larger instances are however more challenging, and overall
RCSF does not offer much improvement, compared to the best upper bounds
known from CPLEX. The average gap to these upper bounds is however at
0.18%, meaning that RCSF on average results in solution close to the upper
bounds. Considering the difference in computational resources used by CPLEX
and RCSF, these are still considered promising results.
The effect of the different instance characteristics, such as size, fixed cost level
and capacity tightness, on the performance of RCSF is summarised in Table
I.4-I.8 in Appendix I.2. Similarly to the filtering heuristics for the regular fixed-
cost multicommodity flow problem, RCSF performs better when used to solve
instances with relatively low fixed-cost levels. There is also some evidence of the
performance being better for loose capacities as well.
In comparisons to CPLEX, the RCSF heuristic seems to be especially advanta-
geous for instances with high fixed cost levels and loose capacities. For instances
with high fixed cost levels and tight capacities however, the competitive capabil-
ities of RCSF seems to be lacking.
==PAGE:114==
7.3 RINVF
106
7.3
RINVF
The tuning of RINVF resulted in four parameter settings being selected for fur-
ther testing. The four sets of parameter values were selected based on exhibiting
different beneficial traits, when applied to the test-set of instances.
(maxfree, λ, wx)=(90, 0.25, 1) was chosen best on resulting in a good average so-
lution quality.
Similarly, (maxfree, λ, wx)=(90, 0.2, 10) was selected as it had
the best worst solution gap, without significantly increasing the average solu-
tion quality.
Further, (maxfree, λ, wx)=(75, 0.2, 10) proved to have a decent
performance, while also having a greater potential in terms of running-time.
(maxfree, λ, wx)=(80, 0.25, 7) generally resulted in the worst solution qualities
amongst the four selected parameter-sets, but it proved to be one of the fastest
settings also providing decent solution qualities.

performance for the four selected parameter settings chosen for RINVF, when

The first observation is that the worst case
performance by the filtering heuristic alone, is quite poor. These measures are
however significantly improved by applying Local Branching to the solutions ob-
tained by the filtering heuristic.
The best solution qualities from RINVF alone, are found for (maxfree=90, λ=0.25,

==PAGE:115==
7.3 RINVF
107
to perform better for many instances in the filter heuristic phase, as the reduced
MIP solved at the end of RINVF is less restricted. This is however also likely
to result in longer running times for RINVF, which will leave less time for Local
Branching to improve the solution quality.
A counter-example to this theory, is however the fact that (maxfree=90, λ=0.2,
wx=10.0) outperforms (maxfree=80, λ=0.25, wx=7.0) in terms of worst case so-
lution quality, even though they obtain similar solution qualities after RINVF
terminates.
Based on these performance comparisons, the version of RINVF resulting
from the parameter combination (maxfree, λ, wx)=(75, 0.2, 10), is chosen to be
subject to further evaluations and comparisons of performance.

7.4
Comparing methods
The testing of the route-based methods is concluded by a direct comparison of
their performances. The heuristics will be compared based on the chosen param-
eters, to assess the solution quality levels of the chosen versions, i.e. with the
most promising parameter settings.
Further, the best solution found for each instance, amongst all tested parameter
combinations of RCSF and RINVF respectively, will also be considered. The
idea here is to examine the potential of each of the methods from a different
point of view. As the parameter-value sets tested for each of the heuristics, in
the previous sections, are those that seemed to have the greatest potential during
tuning, their combined performance can be used to approximate the best case
performance one can expect to achieve.

==PAGE:117==
7.4 Comparing methods
109
The usual LB and UB gaps are considered, both after the heuristic terminates
and after Local Branching is applied for the remaining time. The last rows gives
the counts of instances for which equal, improving and worse solutions are found,
when compared to the upper bounds provided by CPLEX.
Based on the heuristic performance alone, it appears that RCSF is the best
performing of the methods, as it has the best average and worst case solution
qualities. Applying the route-restricted capacity scaling method on its own, here
clearly results in the poorest solution qualities, This can be seen in the column for
RCSLB. One does however have that these solutions are obtained in a significantly
shorter time than those for RCSF and RINVF. This is a natural consequence of
the other methods being composite heuristics, applying RCS iteratively.
The fact that there is more time available for Local branching in RCSLB, also
explains why the final solution qualities are much closer to those of the other
methods, than for the heuristic solutions. RCSLB is however still the method
with the largest worst case solution gaps, and it is also outperformed in terms of
minimal and average gaps, by RINVF.
RINVF has proven to be the heuristic, out of the three, that results in the best
distribution of solution qualities. One does however also have that this method
results in fewer instances with improved solutions, compared to CPLEX. In fact,
CSLB has the largest number of improved solutions, as well as the lowest number
of worse solutions, even though the overall solution gaps are less promising.

==PAGE:118==
7.4 Comparing methods
110
The results reflect much of the same trends, as stated above. RCSF provides the
overall best average worst case solution qualities before Local branching is used,
but RINVF generally results in the best solution qualities overall. Both RCSF
and RINVF outperform RCSLB, and RCSF now has the best counts in terms of
instances with equal or better solutions. RINVF is also now a close second, when
considering this performance indicator.
As such RINVF seems to be the most promising of the tested methods. It
has the potential to approach the solution quality level obtained by CPLEX,
using significantly fewer computational resources. This is in contrast to the re-
sults obtained from the parameter tuning, where RCSF appeared to be the most
promising method. This raises some doubt about the tuning test-set of instances,
appropriately representing different instance structures and characteristics.
Further, comparing the chosen parameter settings to the overall best solutions,
only the average UB-gap obtained, is reduced from 0.14% to 0.07%. This could
be an indicator of the chosen parameters being appropriate.
Lastly, it is found that the performance of the route-restricted capacity scaling
heuristic is generally poorer than the corresponding method for the original prob-
lem. One also has that RFCMCF would benefit from an intensification procedure,
similar to that used for solving FCMCF, to help obtain better intermediate solu-
tions.
==PAGE:119==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusion
The main focus of this thesis has been on developing new heuristics aimed at
solving the fixed-cost multicommodity flow problem (FCMCF). Promising meth-
ods from the literature have been used as a basis for a set of new hybrid heuris-
tics.
The considered components ranged from meta-heuristic procedures like
cycle-based intensification to LP-search methods like slope scaling and capacity
scaling, as well as the MIP-based soft variable fixing heuristic Local Branching.
The different methods from the literature were adapted to work appropriately for
their intended functions. The adapted versions have been tested in isolation and
have proved to provide favourable results, also when comparing to the original
methods from the literature.
Greater efforts were focused on improving the slope scaling procedure, pro-
posed by Kim and Pardalos [6]. A worst case analysis identified the potential
difficulties of the original method, motivating the development of extended scal-
ing schemes. To this purpose, four new update schemes for slope scaling were
proposed in this thesis. Three of these schemes exhibited promising results in
terms of solution qualities, but the convergence proved to be sloe. After includ-
ing additional speed-up measures it was found that the running times of the
schemes could be reduced significantly while maintaining good solution-quality
levels.
The capacity scaling heuristic, proposed by Katayama et. al. [10], is in this
thesis applied to the edge-formulation of FCMCF, whereas the original method
was concerned with the path-based problem formulation. Testing of the version
proposed in this thesis revealed promising results. Especially, it was found that
when pairing the capacity scaling with the cycle-based intensification procedure
used by Crainic et. al. [13], the obtained performance was competitive with that
of the original method.
The main contributions of this thesis is a set of hybrid heuristics. These new
methods are constructed by different combinations of the procedures adapted
from the literature. The shared fundamental idea is that one can restrict the
MIP-formulation of the problem by identifying common features amongst a set
of good-quality network designs. Ideally, the strategy should restrict the problem
sufficiently, such that the optimal solution to the reduced problem is efficiently
obtained. There is no guarantee that the restricted MIP shares optimal solu-
tion with the full model. However, the hypothesis was that this approach would
increase the likelihood of obtaining near-optimal solutions in a relatively short
running time.
==PAGE:120==
8 Conclusion
112
To help guide some of these proposed heuristics in their iterative search for
suitable intermediate solutions, a new application of core concepts used in in-
verse optimisation was proposed. This approach was concerned with solving an
appropriate inverse optimisation problem to obtain a set of perturbations for the
objective cost-vector parameters. The hypothesis was that these perturbations
could give some indication of the distance to the true optimal solution for the
different components of the current network solution.
By combining different heuristic components, eight filtering methods for FCMCF
were defined. Each of these methods were then subject to parameter tuning and
tested on a wide range of instances, varying in size, fixed cost level, and capacity
tightness. The obtained results were compared to best known solution bounds
from the literature. The results showed that most of the heuristics succeeded in
finding improved solutions for some sub-set of the considered instances. Further,
it was found that two of the filtering heuristics obtained especially promising
solutions. On average they both performed better than the methods from the
literature, even for the very large network problems. Overall, the filtering ap-
proach proved to have favourable capabilities in terms of obtaining good-quality
solutions to FCMCF within a reasonable time frame.
Less promising were the results obtained by the inverse optimisation-based
methods, as none of the tested variations could outperform the best filtering
heuristics. It was however found that the best results across a sub-set of param-
eters for one of the inverse optimisation-based methods outperformed the same
results for the other heuristics. It is believed that this is an indication of the
method having potential for further developments.
The last parts of this thesis focused on the Route-restricted fixed-cost mul-
ticommodity flow problem (RFCMCF). Two of the heuristics developed for the
regular FCMCF were adapted to handle this extended problem and then tuned
and tested accordingly. There were no available bounds for RCFMCF in the liter-
ature which made conclusions about the heuristic performances less definite. To
obtain some basis for comparison, a set of upper and lower bounds was obtained
from CPLEX. The results for RFCMCF showed that the proposed heuristics on
average approached the same solution quality levels as obtained by CPLEX, using
significantly fewer computational resources in terms of CPU-time. It was found
that out of the two heuristics, the inverse optimisation-based method performed
best for this version of the problem. It did however become evident that fur-
ther developments of intensification and scaling procedures for solving RCFMCF
would be beneficial.
The overall conclusions from the work in this thesis is that the filtering ap-
proach used in the proposed heuristics overall seems promising. Multiple methods
utilising this concept, but employing different heuristic components, have proven
to have reasonably good performances. There are some indications of the new
use of inverse optimisation possessing beneficial traits, but there is also evidence
suggesting further research is needed.

Figure B.2: Average performance of the intensification procedure, when applied
to the solutions found during capacit scaling.
Figure B.2 shows the average percentage solution improvement when the ca-
pacity scaling method is paired with Cycle-based intensification, as well as the
average additional running time. Again, the intensification procedure is found
to have limited effect on the computational time, and to be better at improving
lower-quality solutions. It is clear that the method uses more time to achieve
a smaller relative improvement, when the solution provided is already of good
quality.

C.1.1
TYPE I initialisation scheme
In this section a detailed description of the iterative behaviour of the slope scaling
method using a TYPE I initialisation scheme for the problem (2.55) is considered.
Case (i)
In this case the optimal solution is to choose the overall cheapest of
the paths, and send all flow along this path. Due to the initial slope factor vector
for the TYPE I initial, it will here be assumed that c1 < c2. This is without loss
of generality, as the converse would simply mirror the following effects of running
the procedure.
1. ¯c0 = (c1, c2) ⇒x0 = (d, 0)

As such the slope scaling procedure will always choose the optimal solution in
this scenario.
Case (ii)
The problem is here not symmetric as one path has a capacity strictly
smaller than the demand, whereas the other has a capacity which is at least
equal to the demand. Therefore both cases c1 < c2 and c2 < c1 here needs to be
examined separately.

C.1 Two possible paths

C.1.3
Three paths
Now, the case where three possible (ˆs, ˆt)-paths exists will be considered, and the
optimal solution to the single-commodity flow network design problem is then
formalised by
x∗=arg min{c1x1 + f1y1 + c2x2 + f2y2 + c3x3 + f3y3 |
x1 + x2 + x3 = d, x1 ≤u1y1, x2 ≤u2y2,
x3 ≤u3y3, x1, x2, x3 ≥0, y1, y2, y3 ∈{0, 1}}
(C.39)
As the problem has grown in size, the number of different cases, defined by
the problem parameters, have also become greater. This section will therefore
attempt at deducing the worst case behaviour without testing each possible case.
It is firstly noted that if all paths can carry the total demand, individually, then
one can easily confirm, as an extension of the case with two paths, that the slope
scaling procedure will find the optimal solution. This means that the worst case
behaviour occurs when at least one of the possible paths has a capacity strictly
smaller than the demand.
The previous tests have further shown how the slope scaling procedure fails
when an over-estimated marginal cost is allocated to an edge, forcing the pro-
cedure to send less than optimal flow along this edge. This is obtained when a
smaller fraction of the demand is routed along the edge, at some iteration.
As stated before, the cases where the obtained solution uses totally different edges
to the true optimal solution are the most problematic. Therefore, this worst case
analysis will focus on cases where edges that are a crucial part of the optimal
solution, are pushed out of basis by the estimated slope factors.
Given that the optimal solution is to choose exactly one edge, the worst case is
that the estimate solution distributes the demand across the two other available
edges instead. If the optimal solution is to split demand amongst two edges, the
worst case will be to obtain a solution were the edge, of these two, with the lowest
unit flow cost is excluded.
C.1.3.1
Single path optimal solution
Consider the problem (C.39) where the optimal solution is given x∗= (d, 0, 0) and
the capacities are defined u1 ≥d, u2 = αd and u3 = βd. As a capacity strictly
smaller than demand is needed for at least one edge, require that α ∈]0, 1[, and
α + β ≥1 to ensure feasibility of the desired non-optimal solution.
For the given x∗to be optimal, it needs to be true that
dc1 + f1 < γdc2 + τdc3 + f2 + f3
(C.40)
for all γ, τ ∈[0, 1] such that γ + τ = 1, γ ≤α and τ ≤β.
This analysis will start with the TYPE II initialisation scheme, as this scheme
had the best overall worst-case bound for the two path cases.
==PAGE:145==
C.1 Two possible paths
137
TYPE II initial
To make the slope scaling procedure distribute less flow along
edge x1 than the optimal, the first iterate could take the form x0 = (d−u2, u2, 0),
for this to happen, it needs to hold that
c2 + f2
u2
< c1 + f1
u1
and c1 + f1
u1
< c3 + f3
u3
(C.41)
Then, in the next iteration, it is desirable to redirect flow away from x1, to move
away from the optimal solution. As x0
2 = u2 and x3 = 0 the slope factor for
these edges remains the same, and thus while that of x1 increases, as d−u2 < u1.
Therefore, the only option for moving flow away from x1 is to reroute it through
x3, which is done if
c3 + f3
u3
< c1 +
f1
d −u2
(C.42)
and one obtains the iterate x1 = (0, u2, d −u2).
To avoid any resulting flow along x1, the procedure should now terminate at this
solution. For this to happen, the condition
c3 +
f3
d −u2
< c1 +
f1
d −u2
(C.43)
needs to hold true.
The found solution is thus xss = (0, u2, d −u2). Given that the true optimal
was x∗= (d, 0, 0), the worst case optimality gap is now investigated.
The marginal cost of the slope scaling procedure is given
zss
d = αc2 + (1 −α)c3 + f2
d + f3
d
(C.44)
and based on the conditions to obtain this solution, one can deduce that
c2 + f2
u2
< c1 + f1
u1
⇒αc2 + f2
u2
< αc1 + α f1
u1
(C.45)
c3 +

d −u2

d < (1 −α)c1 + f1
d
(C.46)
(C.47)
which means that the marginal cost can be bounded from above, in terms of the
true optimal value
zss

u1
+ (1 −α)c1 + f1

+ f1
d
(C.48)
= c1 + 2f1

d
(C.49)
The worst case scenario is thus bounded by a factor two, compared to the optimal.
==PAGE:146==

sation scheme actually means that a single path, different from the optimal, can
be chosen.
The found solution is thus xss = (0, 0, d). Given that the true optimal was
x∗= (d, 0, 0), the worst case optimality gap is now investigated.
The marginal cost of the slope scaling procedure is given
zss

(preject, ndiv)=(20, 20) being the most beneficial, as this pair results in the best
average case and second best worst case.
As such the best settings seem to be when preject>0.1 and ndiv > 1.

Table E.1 gives a performance summary of the average and worst-case perfor-
mance for each point in the parameter-grid. Presented are maximal and average
time usage, as well as gap of final solution. In addition, the worst and best solu-
tion counts for each parameter-combination, within this method, are given. These
are computed by counting for how many of the tested instances the respective
parameter-combination locates the best solution found across all parameter-pairs
for SSF. An instance may be counted twice here, as several parameter settings
may lead to the same solution value.
One can observe that for both value-pair (preject, ndiv)=(0.2, 3) and (0.3, 20),
the procedure experiences time-out for at least one instance. These combinations
also have a lesser performance wrt. worst case solution quality. Generally, this
version of the filtering procedure converges in a short amount of time, as desired.
The best worst-case performance is observed for preject=0.2.
Amongst these,
time-out is only relevant for ndiv=3, which also has the worst average performance
and highest number of worst solutions.
Figure E.2 illustrates the distribution of free edges after the filtering is fin-
ished, and of the percentage of the running time used in the filtering part of the
procedure. This last measure is the percentage determined by what part of the
total running time which is used in the iterative filtering phase. Conversely, the
remaining percentages of the running time are used to solve the restricted MIP
and intensifying its soution, in the last phase.
Interestingly almost all parameter combinations result in a number of free edges
above the threshold of 75. As there are few cases of time-out of the procedure,
this characteristic is not detrimental to the performance of SSF.
Parameters (preject, ndiv)=(0.2, 3) spans the largest counts of free edges, which
is also likely connected to the lower fraction of time used in the filtering step.
This property is likely also the reason for the recorded time-out for this combi-
nation.
The reason for time-out of (preject, ndiv)=(0.3, 20) is less prominent, as the
number of free edges is generally closer to the threshold and the fraction of time
used in filtering is similar to the general level of all parameters. This combination
does however have one of the smaller average running times, which may indicate
==PAGE:154==

that few of the instances forced the method to time out.

Table E.2 summarises the two instances where SSF has experienced time-out.
An instance is characterised by the dataset name, the parameter settings used.
For each, the number of free edges resulting from the iterative filtering and the
number of iterations are reported. The iteration filter time is given in both sec-
onds and percentage of total time. Gap refers to the usual percentage measure
for solution quality.
One can observe that both are due to exceeding one hour, and not due to inef-
ficient filtering, as the times used for the iterative part of the procedure is about
18% in both cases. Further, the actual time-out is of little relative magnitude,
but large enough to indicate a possible early termination of the final branch-and-
bound.
The reason for the time-out is likely due to the large numbers of free edges after
time-out, as both greatly exceeds the threshold of 75. This could be an indicator
of the diversification being too effective, as the number of free edges has increased
from <75 after the second iteration, to 98 and 128 respectively after the third
iteration. For the first instance the likely cause is the large value of preject, which
==PAGE:155==
E.2 Initial tuning of SSS1F
147
controls the degree of diversification, while for the second instance, the use of the
stronger intensification in the third iteration.

the smaller smoothing parameter-value.
Further, this clear distinction could be an indicator that the quality of the inter-
mediate solutions are of importance, as the smaller smoothing parameter proved
to have better solutions, when SSS1 was tested in isolation.

Table E.3 presents the average and worst case behaviour of SSS1F for λ=0.4.
The worst and best counts presented are based on all results across both λ-values,
and as such, the presence of only one worst found solution, further amplifies that
λ=0.4 is the most beneficial choice. The full table across both λ-values is given
in Table E.11 in Appendis E.6.2.2.
It can be observed that the parameters (0.4, 0.3, 3), which results in the most
noteworthy solution qualities, have a maximal time exceeding one hour. This
means that the procedure here has timed-out for at least one instance, which is
seen as a less desirable feature.
Otherwise, most parameters lead to an efficient procedure, which is promising for
further tuning of the method.
Figure E.4: Effect of diversification and time-usage of SSS1F across parameter
combinations.
Figure E.4 shows the parameter-dependent distributions of number of free
edges and filtering time-usage, respectively. These results can be used to assess
==PAGE:157==
E.3 Initial tuning of SSS3F
149
the effect of the different diversification settings.
A general observation is that the number of free edges is strongly linked to preject.
For small preject there are several cases where the threshold is not reached before
the filtering terminates, whereas with a higher parameter value, the threshold is
exceeded in most cases.
There is also a notable relation between the number of free edges and the per-
centage time used in filtering. It becomes clear that more free edges lead to less
time being used in filtering, thus implying that a larger fraction of the running
time is used in the final branch-and-bound.
These correlations are crucial also for the best solution parameters (0.4, 0.3, 3),
which clearly has the lowest fraction of time used for filtering. Therefore, a slow
final branch-and-bound is likely the culprit, causing the recorded time-out.
Table E.12 in Appendix E.6.2.3 shows the 22 cases of time-outs for the SSS1F
method. The 16 first cases are all due to reaching the upper iteration limit of
20, before 75 free edges are obtained. The entire procedure otherwise converges
quickly, as the total running time is usually less than 1000 seconds.
The 17th, 18th and 21st cases have similar traits, where time-out of the fil-
tering procedure is caused by reaching the prescribed time-limit. In these cases
the number of resulting free-edges is thus also lower than the desired threshold.
After freeing an extra set of edges to reach this threshold, the branch-and-bound
converges quickly, as 80-90% of the total time is used in the filtering.
One has that (λ, preject)=(0.4, 0.1) in all three of the cases, which can attribute
to the slow filtering. It is known that the scaling method, SSS1, is slower for
smaller λ, and also a small preject means the degree of diversification in each
iteration is low, resulting in fewer edges being guided out of basis at a time.
The remaining three cases time out during the branch-and-bound, likely due
to the high number of free edges. This again seems to be a consequence of either
a higher preject of usage of the strong diversification criterion.

Figure E.5: Comparing solution quality across the parameter grid for SSS3F).
Figure E.5 gives a summary of the solution qualities obtained across the
parameter-grid of SSS3F. Firstly, one can note that, in contrast to SSS1F, there

located in the same parameter-region. The best performing preject seems to be
located about 0.2, and stretching towards 0.3, especially considering average per-
formance. The relation between solution quality and ndiv is more unclear.
For λ=0.4 one has that ndiv > 1 seems somewhat more beneficial, whereas preject
either above or below 0.2 performs the best.

E.4 Initial tuning of SSS4F
151
Table E.4 summarises the performance of some of the most promising parameter-
combinations of SSS3F. One can observe that most of the better-performing pa-
rameter combinations time-out for at least one instance. Especially, there is a
clear link between the best average solution qualities and these time-outs. Con-
sidering worst case solution quality however, (0.9, 0.2, 1) is the best performing,
and also has a worst case running time well within the upper time-limit.
Figure E.10 and E.11 in Appendix E.6.3.2 shows the parameter-dependant
distributions of number of free edges and percentage time used for the iterative
filtering for SSS3F. The overall trends across the parameter-grid are similar to
those of SSS1F in Figure E.4. There is some correlation between a relatively long
filtering time and few free edges, as well as between the number of free edges and
the degree of diversification given by the parameter settings.
Amongst the test-cases for SSS3F there are 49 total cases of time-out. These
cases are all summarised in Table E.16 in Appendix E.6.3.2.
Most cases, 34, are instances where the maximal iteration limit is reached. Within
these, two exceed the filtering time-cap as well, and three experience an overall
procedure time-out. Only two of the three time-outs are due to having more than
75 free edges, while the last is due to slow filtering.
This leaves 15 cases where the iteration limit is not reached. Common for
these is that there is no time-out of the iterative filtering, and that the number
of free edges for most is above 75.
Figure E.12 summarises the time-out cases of the method, where λ=0.4 is
used. To the left, the number of instances experiencing time-out is given, for
each point in the parameter-grid. The left plot sketches the distribution of num-
ber of free edges after diversification, also grouped by parameter settings.
It is clear that most cases of time-out are due to a small preject, resulting in a
weaker diversification, which causes the filtering to terminate before the desired
number of free edges is reached.
The remaining few cases are characterised by stronger diversification, a high num-
ber of free edges, and thus are likely to exceed the total runtime limit.
Figure E.12 summarises the same time-out characteristics for λ=0.9.
The
same observations as for λ=0.4 holds here.
Generally, there is again a trend that a high preject leads to a high number of free
edges quickly. Further, when string diversification is applied every third iteration,
i.e. ndiv=3 and preject>0.1 the filtering terminates after the third iteration with
a large number of free edges.

Table E.5 gives a summary of the performance of the parameter-region where
λ=0.2. It can be noticed that the method is generally efficient, with only one
parameter combination leading to time-out. This combination is (0.2, 0.3, 3),
which has a good average performance, but is outperformed by (0.2, 0.3, 20) wrt.
worst case solution quality. This final combination is the most preferable pre-
sented considering solution quality, and further, there are no test which reached
the one hour limit.
Figure E.15 in Appendix E.6.4.3 shows the parameter-dependent distributions
of number of free edges and filtering time-usage for SSS4F with λ=0.2. The usual
relationship between the size of preject and number of free edges is clearly also a
==PAGE:161==
E.5 Initial tuning of CSF
153
factor here, as well as how the percentage time used for filtering decreases with
number of free edges. One does however also have that the majority of cases have
number of free edges close to the threshold.
For individual cases of time-out Table E.20 in Appendix E.6.4.3 shows that
there are 26 relevant cases when considering time-out for SSS4F. Most cases are
due to reaching the iteration limit, which again seems to be related to a weaker
intensification level. If the iteration count limit is disregarded, only three cases
are left.
The first of these is due to a time-out of the filtering procedure, leaving <75 free
edges. This consequence can again be linked to a small preject.
The two last cases both experience time-out in the branch-and-bound, due to a
large number of free edges obtained by string diversification in the third iteration.
Interestingly, these cases also share a high preject, indicating that SSS4F likely
is more stable when using stronger diversification, as only the absolute highest
tested degree of diversification results in any time-out of the entire procedure.

of the previous methods.

The larger λ-value is generally more efficient in terms of computational time,
but the solution quality is also typically a little poorer, especially considering

re-iterating the fact that the CS scheme is slower.
==PAGE:163==
E.5 Initial tuning of CSF
155
Figure E.8: Effect of diversification and time-usage of CSF(B=75) across pa-
rameter combinations with fixed λ=0.4.
Figure E.8 shows the usual distribution-plots of number of free edges and
fraction of time used for filtering time, for the CSF procedure with λ=0.4.
For a small preject the CSF method in most cases does not reach the threshold for
free edges within the current filtering time-limits. This is likely the consequence
of a less intense effect from the diversification in each iteration.
It also becomes clear that the CSF is more stable than the other methods, in
terms of the number of free edges after filtering. There are fewer extreme values
which are also smaller in magnitude, compared to the other methods.
The fact that the method stays well within the hour in all of the conducted test,
also means that the effect of having more free edges is less detrimental to the
convergence rate of the final branch-and-bound. In a vast majority of the cases,
the filtering time constitutes more than 95% of the total running time. This also
indicates a very efficient final branch-and-bound, which again implies that the
CSF filters the edges in such a way, that the final problem is easier to solve.
There are no cases for CSF where the procedure exceeds the total limit of one
hour. There are however 88 cases where the iterative filtering times-out at fewer
than 75 free edges. These cases are all listed in Table E.28 in Appendix E.6.6.
Common for all except 15 cases is that the time-out is due to the upper time-limit
on the iterative filtering. In the last 15 cases, the upper iteration limit is reached,
without exceeding the time-limit. The maximal iteration limit is only reached
with λ=0.9, and nine of the cases occur for c39, the smallest considered dataset.
Figure E.16 in Appendix E.6.6 summarises the time-out cases of the method,
where λ=0.4 is used. The left plot shows how there is a direct relation between
the number of occurrences and the level of diversification used. This is seen as
the point in the parameter-grid with the largest number of such cases is that with
the least diversification, (preject, ndiv) = (0.1, 20), and that the lowest number
of cases is found for the strongest diversification, at (preject, ndiv) = (0.3, 1).
To the right, one can also clearly see that the number of free edges obtained,
==PAGE:164==
E.6 Detailed tuning results
156
increases with the level of diversification.
Figure E.16 in Appendix E.6.6 similarly summarises the time-out cases of
the method using λ=0.9.
The same trends as previously are seen here, but
the difference in number of free edges between the diversification-levels, is less
prominent.

E.7 Finishing rounds of tuning
184
E.7.3
SSS3F
E.7.3.1
Comparing λ-values
Figure E.23: Comparing performance across the range of λ-values.
Figure E.23 illustrates the distribution of solution quality, time usage and num-
ber of free edges after filtering for each λ-value, pooling the two tested values
for preject. One can observe that λ=0.1 has the lowest median gap, and this
distribution also is the most skewed towards zero. However, due to the outlier
with gap above 4%, it also records one of the higher maximal gaps. In terms of
maximal gap λ=0.9 is the most beneficial.
The time-usage distributions shows that there is no pattern of smaller λ-values
leading to slower computational times. The range of this distribution varies across
the λ-values, and in fact, the larger λ-values are heavier towards the longer run-
ning times.
Considering number of free edges, there are a few outliers which lead to larger
degrees of freedom, but generally most tests for all λ-values lead to a somewhat
restricted number of free edges.

Figure E.24: Average and worst-case performance across the tested parameter-
grid.
Figure E.24 displays the average and worst case performance in terms of solu-
tion gap and running time, across the (λ, preject) parameter-grid. The maximal
time used is similar for most combinations, where all seem affected by time-out of
some of the instances. Based on average time however, most instances converges
within a decent running time, across the parameter-grid.
Considering solution quality, there is a clear discrepancy between the average and
worst-case performances, as λ=0.1, which has the significantly smallest average
gap, has a medium worst case performance. λ=0.3 is clearly the best in terms of
worst-case solution quality, but the average measure is in the higher end amongst
the values tested.
Based on this, it is still somewhat unclear within which range λ should be
chosen, as it depends highly on what measure one prioritises. With respect to
preject, it is however clear that larger values are preferred across all of the most
promising λ-values.

E.7 Finishing rounds of tuning

tested problem instances. This information adds to the conflict in choosing ap-
propriate λ-values, as there is a clear discrepancy between the best average and
best worst case performances. Further, λ=0.1 has the best average performance,
likely linked to the four cases of best found solutions, and zero worst case solu-
tions. The maximal gap does however reflect that there are several other λ-values
which performs better.
Due to this inconsistency between the λ-values, and the fact that in general
there are several values which seem promising, the next tuning steps will consider
multiple, distant values of this parameter, while tuning the diversification control-
parameters.

APPENDIX G
Analysing heuristic
performances
G.1
CSLB
CSLB consists of Capacity Scaling with the usual B=75, followed by the cycle-
based intensification procedure, and then Local branching using M=10, δM=2
and T=1000 seconds. The method is tested using λ=0.05.
The instance specific results for CSLB applied to all Canad-instances are given
in Table J.1-J.3 in Appendix J.1.1.
It is found that for λ=0.05, 166 of the 196 considered instances, the solutions
obtained are either equally as good, or better than the upper bounds reported in
the literature. Within this subset of instances 14 belong to the C-category, 9 to
the C+-category and 143 to the R-category.
Instance specific results for the GT-instances are given in Table J.4 in Ap-
pendix J.1.2. Compared to the best upper bounds known from the literature,
there are no instances within this category, for which CSLB locates an improved
solution.
Table G.1 summarises the performance of CSLB, when applied to the Canad-
instances. The solution qualities are evaluated by the UB-gap, to also indicate
how they compare to the best known results from the literature. CS+CI refers
to the performance of pairing capacity scaling with cycle-based intensification,
while CSLB denotes the full method.
Table G.1: Summarising the performance of CSLB when applied to the Canad-
instances, before and after Local Branching.
CS+CI
CSLB

==PAGE:219==
G.1 CSLB
211
The results for CS+CI exhibit a reasonably good average solution quality,
while keeping the running times relatively short. It is however also clear that
there are instances for which the performance is rather poor, as the overall max-
imal UB-gap is 15.59%.
The benefit of the shorter running times is that after applying Local Branching,
overall good quality solutions are obtained. There are still instances for which
the obtained solutions are significantly worse than those found by Katayama
et al. The fact that CSLB has been restricted by one hour of running time is
however also crucial to consider. The solutions to the more challenging instances
from the literature, are obtained using an average running time exceeding 5 hours.
As such, based on the final results, one has that the overall performance is
competitive, when compared to the path-formulation based capacity scaling used
in the literature, as the overall average solution gap is 0%. Further, as CSLB was
restricted to run for only one hour, the maximal running times are significantly
shorter, than those reported in the literature.
Table H.10-H.12 in Appendix H.3.1 presents the performance of CSLB(λ=0.05),
grouped by the different instance characteristics. The instances are each defined
by their size, as well as fixed cost level, relative to the unit-flow costs, and capac-
ity tightness.
The results show that instances with a high fixed cost levels are the most chal-
lenging for CSLB. It is also for these harder instances, where CSLB performs
worse than the heuristic proposed Katayama et al. [11].

Table G.2 gives a performance summary of CSLB(λ=0.05) when applied to
the GT-instances. Both results before and after applying Local Branching are
reported. Further, the performance measures are considered both grouped by
instance specifics, and considering all instances at once.
Firstly, it can be noticed that the difference in solution qualities before and after
Local Branching is limited, especially in terms of the worst case solution gaps.
This is likely due to the size of the instances considered, as larger problems will
==PAGE:220==
G.2 SSF
212
require significantly more iterations and longer running times to complete the
Capacity Scaling. There is then a risk that the time-limit of one hour is reached
before the Local Branching phase has started.
The overall large LB-gaps are not too concerning, as the lower bounds known
for these instances are rather weak. The UB-gap however, does indicate that the
solution quality on average is significantly worse than that obtained by Hewitt
et al. [5], when testing their IP search heuristic. The results of CSLB, for the
large GT-instances, are as such not competitive with those found in the literature,
but the large negative gaps to the best solutions found by CPLEX within the 12
hours, are seen as positive indications.
G.2
SSF
Based on the tuning results of SSF, two parameter settings were chosen for fur-
ther investigations. (preject, ndiv)=(0.35, 1) was chosen due to having the best
worst case solution quality amongst the test-instances.
Due to resulting in a
good average solution gap, (preject, ndiv)=(0.31, 1) was also chosen. This second
parameter combination further resulted in the second best worst case solution
quality for SSF, and the convergence was relatively fast.
SSF is tested for both parameter combinations chosen during tuning. For each
instance, if SSF terminates before the time-limit of one hour is reached, Local
Branching is applied to fill the time, starting from the obtained solution.
G.2.1
comparing parameters
The instance specific results for the different parameter settings of SSF are given
in Tables J.5-J.12 in Appendix J.2-J.3.

Table G.3 compares the average and worst case performance of SSF for the
two selected parameter combinations. The results are compared both before and
after Local Branching is applied. In addition, the number of improved solutions
is reported. This row refers to the number of Canad-instances, out of 196, for
which the obtained solutions are at least as good as those reported by Katayama
et al. [11].
It is evident that the performance of SSF alone, is approximately equal for the two
parameter combinations. One does however have that (preject, ndiv)=(0.31, 1)
results in significantly better final solutions. This is likely tied to the fact that
the average time-usage of SSF using these parameters, is much shorter, leaving
more time for Local Branching.

Table G.4 similarly compares the performance of the two parameter-sets, when

Branching.

SSF locates solutions about equally as good as those found by Katayama et al.

G.3
SSS1F
SSS1F has been tested on all Canad- and GT-instances using each of the three
parameter-sets chosen during tuning. Local Branching is applied to the obtained
solution, if less than one hour is used to complete SSS1F for an instance.
First, the performance of the different versions of the heuristic resulting from the
different parameter settings, will be compared. The first parameter combination
considered is (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.2, 0.24, 4).
These settings are of interest, as
they in the tuning resulted in the lowest average solution gap, while also having
one of the better worst case solution qualities. The second parameter setting,
(λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.25, 0.23, 4) was chosen due to resulting in the best worst case
solution quality amongst the test-instances. Lastly, (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.3, 0.23, 5)
as it had the fastest running time, while still resulting in relatively good solution
qualities.

Table G.8 similarly compares the performance of SSS1F using the different pa-
rameter settings, when applied to the larger GT-instances. Here (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.3, 0.23, 5)
results in the best final solutions, even though it results in the longest average
running time for SSS1F. The quality of the final solutions clearly depends on that
obtained during the filtering heuristic, as the different parameter-combinations
ranks equally for both measures.
This effect is likely related to the fact that the filtering heuristic is rather slow for
such large instances, leaving little to no time for improvement by Local Branching.
One as such has that (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.2, 0.24, 4) has the best performance
for the Canad-instances, while (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.3, 0.23, 5) is the immediate
best parameter-set when solving the GT-instances. However, Figure H.4 in Ap-
pendix H.5.1 shows the full distribution of solution qualities for the GT-instances,
for each of the different parameter settings. One can here observe that the worst
case solution for (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.2, 0.24, 4) is an outlier, and that otherwise
the main distribution does not span as large UB gaps as (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.3, 0.23, 5).
Further, (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.25, 0.23, 4) seems more beneficial when considering
the full gap-distribution. It is found that there are two outliers with especially
large UB-gaps, but that the main body of the distribution skews towards im-
proved solutions.
Therefore, it is difficult to conclude what parameters should be chosen for fur-
ther performance comparisons. It is however decided to continue with (λ, preject, ndiv)
=(0.2, 0.24, 4), as it was the best performing for the Canad-instances, and the dif-
ferences in performance were greater for these instances, when considering the
full distribution.
