Table G.9 summarises the performance, in terms of solution qualities, ob-
tained by applying SSS1F(λ=0.2, preject=0.24, ndiv=4)to the Canad-instances.
The overall average UB-gap is -0.02%, indicating an average improvement in so-
lution quality, compared to the hybrid method used by Katayama et al. [11].
The minimum UB-gap of -3.85% has some positive indications, as this means
significant improvements have been found for some of the R-category instances.
The fact that the maximal UB-gap is approximately 2.4% does however mean
that there are instances for which SSS1F identifies significantly worse solutions
than those presented in the literature.
On average SSS1F performs worst for the larger C+-category problems, whereas
the best overall performance occurs for the R-category. This latter category also
includes the absolute largest set of instances, and represents the greatest network
size diversity.
Tables H.18-H.22 in Appendix H.5.2 considers the performance of SSS1F,
grouped by the different instance characteristics. Firstly, it is noted that also
SSS1F seems to be less adapt at handling high fixed-cost levels. With regards
to the capacity tightness however, there are some conflicting results. When con-
sidering the C- and R-category instances, there is some evidence that SSS1F
performs better for instances with tight capacities, especially in comparison to
the solutions from the literature.
For the C+-category instances however, the absolute worst performance occurs
for the larger instances with high fixed-cost levels and tight constraints. It is also
within this sub-group of instances, the SSS1F performs worst overall, both com-
pared to the best known lower bound, and solutions from the literature. What is
also a distinct trait for this sub-group, is that the average running time used by
==PAGE:227==
G.4 SSS3F
219
Katayama et.al. [11] to obtain the best known upper bounds, exceeds five hours.
In comparison, the running times of SSS1F barely exceeds the one hour.

Table G.10 summarises the performance of the chosen version of SSS1F, when

G.4
SSS3F
SSS3F has been tested on all Canad- and GT-instances using each of the four
parameter-sets chosen during tuning. Local Branching is applied to the obtained
solution is less than one hour is used to complete SSS3F for an instance.
The tuning results showed that the parameter settings for SSS3F resulting in the
best solution qualities, generally had slow running times. (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.95, 0.18, 5)
was chosen based on resulting in the best maximal solution gap, and (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.1, 0.32, 20)
was chosen due to resulting in a good average solution quality, but both settings
proved to not be very time-efficient. (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.9, 0.18, 2) was signifi-
cantly faster, and provided the second best worst case solution quality amongst
all tested parameter-values, but the average performance was worse than for the
two first settings.
Finally, (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.1, 0.25, 20) was chosen based on resulting in some of
the best solution qualities amongst the more efficient parameter combinations.

Comparing parameters
The instance specific results for the different parameter settings of SSS1F are
given in Tables J.25-J.40 in Appendix J.7-J.10.

Table G.12 similarly compared the performance of the different SSS3F-versions
when tested on the GT-instances. One can here observe that (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.1, 0.25, 20)
results in the best solution quality level, both before and after Local Branching.
One again has that the running times of the filtering heuristic is slow for each
of the parameter settings, meaning the final obtained solution quality is highly
dependant upon the performance of SSS3F alone.
One therefore has some contradictory results for SSS3F as the parameter val-
ues defined by (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.95, 0.18, 5) clearly has the best performance
for the Canad-instances, while (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.1, 0.25, 20) is significantly bet-
ter than the rest, when applied to the GT-instances. One also has that these
two parameter-setting are far apart in the parameter-grid, further adding to the
uncertainty about which should be considered the best.
It can however be observed that the performance for the GT-instances is signifi-
cantly poorer than all considered parameters for SSS1F. Therefore, SSS3F is not
likely to be beneficial when applied to these larger networks.
The parameter setting (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.95, 0.18, 5) is therefore chosen for fur-
ther performance analysis, as it is with these parameter-values SSS3F has the
largest competitive potential.

Table G.13 summarises the performance in terms of solution qualities of SSS3F(λ=0.95,
preject=0.18, ndiv=5) across the different groupings of Canad-instances.
The
overall solution quality is promising, as the average UB gap is small negative,
implying that on average, the heuristic is at least as good as the capacity scaling
proposed by Katayama et al. [11].

larger R-category instances, and the negative average gap also indicates good
performance. One does however also have that it is for these instances the maxi-
mal LB-gap is the largest.

reported in the literature, is the smallest. The average and minimum UB-gaps
do however indicate that the performance is poorer than for the heuristic used
in the literature, and no improved solutions are found within this category.
The larger C+-instances constitutes the most challenging instances for SSS3F. It
is also within this group the worst solution pool is obtained, compared to the
literature.
Table H.23-H.27in Appendix H.6.2 considers the relations between perfor-
mance of SSS3F and the different instance characteristics. There is overall con-
sensus that SSS3F performs best for instances with low fixed-cost levels. With
regards to the effect of capacity tightness, the results differ for the different cate-
gories of Canad-instances. The results for the R-category instances indicate that
capacity tightness has little effect on performance, while for the C-category and
C+-category instances, this characteristic is of importance. One has that for the
C-category instances, looser capacities seem to be the most challenging, especially
when considering the LB gaps. For the C+-category instances however, SSS3F
is at a disadvantage when solving instances with tight capacities.

Table G.14 summarises the performance of SSS3F on the GT-instances, and how
the solution qualities depend on the characteristics of a problem instance. The
performance is clearly better for the sub-set of instances with tight capacities.
Within this subset, there is at least one instance for which the obtained solution is
a 5% improvement, compared to the best known upper bounds from the literature.
The overall average gap to the upper bounds is however large enough to indicate
that SSS3F performs worse than the IP search proposed by Hewitt et al. [5].
G.5
SSS4F
SSS4F has been tested on all Canad- and GT-instances using each of the three
parameter-sets chosen during tuning. Local Branching is applied to the obtained
solution is less than one hour is used to complete SSS4F for an instance.
The first considered parameter setting, (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.2, 0.25, 5), proved to
have the best worst case solution quality and a decent average performance dur-
ing tuning.
The running times were however relatively slow for this setting.
(λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.2, 0.25, 4) was one of the fastest parameter combinations over-
all, while also resulting in the second best maximal solution gap. The drawback
for this setting is however that the average performance proved to be less promis-
ing. The last parameter setting, (λ, preject, ndiv)=(0.2, 0.35, 4), resulted in the
absolute best average solution quality, but was less promising in terms of the
other performance measures.
G.5.1
Comparing parameters
The instance specific results for the different parameter settings of SSS1F are
given in Tables J.41-J.52 in Appendix J.11-J.13.

resulted in 164 instances having equally as good or improved solutions, compared
to the literature. This subset consists of 14 C-category instances, 10 C+-category
instances and 140 R-category instances.

Table G.17 summarises the performance of SSS4F(λ=0.2, preject=0.35, ndiv=4)
in terms of solution qualities across the different groupings of the Canad-instances.
The overall performance is very promising with a LB-gap of about 0.5%, and a
small negative UB gap. These results means that SSS4F succeeds in finding the
optimal or near optimal solution for a majority of the instances, and that on
overage is offers a small improvement compared to the capacity scaling proce-
dure implemented by Katayama et al. [11].
As for several of the other filtering heurstics tested, the performance is best for
the R-category problems, where new best upper bounds are found. The larger
C+-category instances are also for SSS4F the most challenging, but the results
still have an average distance of only 0.6% to those reported in the literature.
Table H.28-H.32 in Appendix H.7.2 displays the instance-characteristics de-
pendent performance of SSS4F. Common for all categories of Canad-instances,
is that the obtained solution quality is better for instances with low fixed-cost
levels.
Within the C- and R-category instances, one has that the performance of SSS4F
is better for loose capacities if the fixed-cost level is low. Conversely, if the fixed
cost level is high, then generally better solutions are obtained for instances with
tight capacities.
For the C+-category instances, one has that it is the larger
instances with high fixed-cost levels and tight capacities, which are the most
challenging.

G.6
CSF
Based on the results from the parameter tuning, four parameter-sets were chosen
for further testing of CSF. (λ, preject, ndiv) = (0.35, 0.18, 40) was chosen due to re-
sulting in the overall best solution qualities. The second setting, (λ, preject, ndiv) =
(0.4, 0.14, 2), came close to the best solution qualities, while being a little faster.
(λ, preject, ndiv) = (0.9, 0.15, 5) also had decent solution qualities, especially con-
sidering average gap, and also proved to be more time-efficient than the first
two. The last parameter combination, (λ, preject, ndiv) = (0.9, 0.2, 3), was chosen
mainly due its computational efficiency. The solution qualities were significantly
worse than the other three chosen sets, but still at a good level compared to the
other faster combinations.
G.6.1
Comparing parameters
The instance specific results for the different parameter settings of SSS1F are
given in Tables J.53-J.68 in Appendix J.14-J.17.

G.7 CINV1F
231
Table G.22 summarises the performance of CSF when applied to the GT-instances.
The overall results indicate a rather poor performance compared to the IP search
proposed in the literature, Hewitt et al. [5]. This can be seen as the average
UB-gap is at 7.71%. The fact that the minimum UB-gap is 0%, means that the
best case for CSF, is to match the solutions found in the literature.
As such, these results indicate that CSF is not well-equipped to handle very large
instances. This is likely tied to the slow iteration times, as capacity scaling is
generally slower than slope scaling. Slower iterations means that fewer interme-
diate solutions are used to determine the final restricted MIP, which in turn is
likely to result in fewer free edges.
G.7
CINV1F
In the tuning phase, four parameter sets were chosen for further testing of
CINV1F. The first, (maxfree, λ, wx)=(100, 0.18, 0.0) was chosen due to provid-
ing the best average and worst case solution gaps for the test-set instances. The
average running time for this parameter combination did however prove to be
slow. (maxfree, λ, wx)=(85, 0.35, 5.0) proved to result in an almost as good aver-
age solution quality, with a significantly shorter average running time. The last
two, (maxfree, λ, wx)=(75, 0.9, 7.0) and (maxfree, λ, wx)=(75, 0.28, 0.0), were se-
lected due to having some of the fastest average running time. The first of these
maintained a good average solution quality, while the second was more beneficial
in terms of worst case solution quality.
G.7.1
Comparing parameters
Instance specific results for each of the four resulting versions of CINV1F can be
found in Table J.69-J.84 in Appendix J.18. Table G.23 compares the four chosen
parameter settings for CINV1F when applied to the Canad-instances, based on
the usual performance indicators. One has that (maxfree, λ, wx)=(100, 0.18, 0.0)
performs the best in terms of average solution qualities, both before and after
Local Branching is applied.
The best worst case solution qualities, after Lo-
cal Branching, are however found for (maxfree, λ, wx)=(75, 0.9, 7.0). This latter
parameter combination is also that which results in the largest count of equally as
good or improved solutions, when compared to the literature. (maxfree, λ, wx)=(100, 0.18, 0.0)
is the second best performing parameter set, when considering this count.

==PAGE:242==
G.8 CINV2F
234
Table H.38-H.42 in Appendix H.9.2 summarises how the different instance
characteristics influence the performance of CINV1F. For the R- and C-category
instances, one has that CINV1F performs worse for instances with relatively high
fixed costs. In terms of capacity levels, the performance is worse for tight capac-
ities if the fixed cost level is low, but the tight capacities are more challenging if
the fixed cost level is high.
Within the R-category, the average performance is generally better than the so-
lution from the literature, for instances with loose capacities. For the C-category
instances however, the overall performance is slightly worse than that reported
in the literature.
Fore the C+-category instances, the most challenging instances are those with
high fixed cost levels and tight capacities. It is also for the larger instances with
these characteristics that the performance is poorest, compared to the literature.
Katayama et al. did however allow for running times exceeding five hours, to
obtain the better performance.

for the IP-search.
G.8
CINV2F
Similarly to CINV1F, four parameter sets were chosen for further testing of
CINV2F. The first setting, (maxfree, λ, wx)=(100, 0.9, ce/fe) was chosen due to
obtaining a very good average solution quality level, for the considered test-
instances.
Similarly, (maxfree, λ, wx)=(100, 0.9, 0.0) was chosen based on the
results having a low maximal solution gap, while also obtaining the second best
average solution quality level.
Both (maxfree, λ, wx)=(100, 0.95, 0.25) and (maxfree, λ, wx)=(75, 0.75, 10.0) were
selected based on being more time-efficient, while still obtaining reasonably good
solutions.

G.8.2
CINV2F(maxfree=100, λ=0.9, wx=0.0)
The test results for CINV2F(maxfree=100, λ=0.9, wx=0.0) shows that 166 of
the 196 Canad-instances either have equally as good or better solutions than the
upper bounds reported by Katayama et al. [11]. This subset consists of 15 C-
category instances, 10 C+-category instances and 141 R-category instances.
For the GT-instances only two improved solutions are obtained, and they are
both for instances with tight capacities.

Table G.29 compares the final results obtained by CINV2F(maxfree=100, λ=0.9,
wx=0.0), to the best lower and upper bounds, known from the literature. The
overall performance is very promising, and both the maximal LB- and UB-gaps

one can also observe that the performance of CINV2F seems to be good overall.

levels and loose capacities.

fixed-cost levels and tight capacities. Further, for the other characteristic-based
sub-groups, one here has that CINV2F matches the performance of the heuristic
proposed by Katayama et al. [11].

G.9
S3INVF
Based on the results obtained in the tuning phase, three parameter settings for
S3INVF were chosen for further investigations. As the overall results showed that
there was some conflict in terms of what parameter combinations had the best
average and the best worst case performances, all three chosen sets were chosen
based in achieving a better balance between the two.
Further, S3INVF was tuned for a fixed maxfree=75. As increasing this thresh-
old has proven to be beneficial in some cases, the parameter combinations chosen
are also tested for maxfree=90. Further, due to the stagnation in number of
free edges obtained, for some of the chosen values of wx a termination criteria is
implemented to stop if this effect is observed. This is especially important when
increasing maxfree, as many instances did not even reach the threshold of 75 free
edges, during tuning.
The new termination criteria states that if there is no change in number of free
edges two iterations in a row, and one the solutions obtained in these two itera-
tions are identical, in terms of objective, then the iterative phase is terminated
prematurely. The hope is that the combination of these two criteria, is a suf-
ficient indicator that no further information can be obtained by continuing the
search.
G.9.1
Comparing parameters
Instance specific results for each of the four resulting versions of S3INVF can be
found in Table J.101-J.112 in Appendix J.26.

Table G.31 compares the different parameter settings for S3INVF, when ap-
plied to the Canad-instances. Firstly, one can observed that the results reported
for (λ, wx)=(0.9, 0.5) and (λ, wx)=(0.925, 0.0) are obtained using maxfree=90,
while maxfree=75 remains for (λ, wx)=(0.935, ce/fe). This is a result from test-
ing each (λ, wx)-pair with both threshold values, and choosing maxfree=90 is
the increase lead to an improved performance, which it did for two of the pairs.
The reason for no observed effect when increasing the threshold when wx=ce/fe,
is likely tied to the rapid stagnation of new free edges per iteration, observed for
this value during tuning.
Firstly, one can observe that the average performance of the different parame-
ter triplets, in terms of final solution qualities, is about the same level. Secondly,
one has that (maxfree, λ, wx)=(90, 0.9, 0.5) has the best overall worst case per-
formance, but (maxfree, λ, wx)=(90, 0.925, 0.0) is the best in terms of equally as
good and improved solutions, when compared to the results form the literature.
An interesting note is that the worst case solution quality is significantly bet-
ter for (maxfree, λ, wx)=(75, 0.925, ce/fe), than the other settings, before Local
Branching is applied. This is obtained even though this version of S3INVF has
fewer free edges in the final restricted MIP, and uses the shortest amount of time.

upper bounds reported by Katayama et al. [11]. This subset consists of 14 C-
category instances, 9 C+-category instances and 139 R-category instances. For
the GT-instances, it is known that there is only one improved solution, achieved
by this version of S3INVF.

Table G.33 summarises the performance of S3INVF(maxfree=90, λ=0.9, wx=0.5)
across the different groupings of the Canad-instances. The overall results show
that S3INVF performs relatively good compared to the literature, as the average
gap to the best known upper bounds is only 0.05%. The fact that the greatest
improvement of solutions is better than the maximal UB-gap, also has promising
implications. The fact that the maximal LB-gap is 6.2% means that there is
however, potential for some solutions being relatively far from optimal.
Considering the R-category instances, it appears that S3INVF obtains solutions
which overall are more promising than those reported in the literature. S3INVF
seems to be more challenged by the C- and C+-category instances, where the
overall performance for the C+-category instances is the poorest, compared to
the literature.

dependent performance of S3INVF, within each sub-category of the Canad-instances.
The overall trends show that S3INVF is especially challenged by instances with
high fixed cost levels, and there is some indication of poorer performance when
the capacities are also tight. S3INVF outperforms Katayama’s heuristic most
R-category sub-groups, with exception of the groups with tight capacities. As
previously discovered, Katayama on average outperforms S3INVF for both the
C- and C+-category instances.
